the Ethiopian and American 
[system of] education?

resembles the American [system] 
in many respects ( 'things'). In 
some respects, however, it is 
different, 

college

at college level is very similar 
to that [given] in America. 

But the education given at 
elementary and secondary 
level is not similar to 
the American education.

How many grades are there in the 
elementary schools? 

There are eight grades ( 'the 
elementary schools are divided 
into eight grades').

designed for one year, eight 
years are required to complete 
the elementary education.

Gramma 1 1 ca 1 Notes 



Note 52.1 Verb: The verb /momulat/ 'to fill 1 used impersonally, as in 

/moltowal/ 'there is plenty', /moltofiflal/ 'I have 
plenty', etc. 



bewasmgton wisT mokina moltowal. 



awo, moltowal. 



There are a lot of cars in 
Wa shmgton. 

Yes, there are plenty. 



The present perfect of the verb /momulat/ 'to fill' may be used imper- 
sonally in expressions (/moltowal/ and /moltowal/) equivalent to the English 
'there is plenty,' 'there are plenty' as illustrated by the above examples. 
The same form used with verb suffix pronouns means 'I have plenty, ' 'you 
have plenty, ' etc. 

Examples: 



sowiyyew I130C moltotal. 
kobtoc moltonnal. 



The man has plenty of children. 
I have a large herd of cattle. 



The paradigm of this form is: 

moltonflal 

moltohal 

moltosal 

moltotal 

raoltwatal 

mo 1 tonal 

mo 1 1 owa c c lhuwa 1 

moltowacc9wal 



'I have plenty' 

'you (m) have plenty' 

'you (i) have plenty' 

'he has plenty' 

'she has plenty' 

'we have plenty ' 

'you (pi.) have plenty' 

'they have plenty'

Grammatical Notes 



Note 54-1 Postposition /sir/ r under f 

ityoPiya y9mmittigg9zzaw b9nugus9 
n9gost silTan sir n9w. 

m9Sihafu k9Tor9Pezaw sir n9w. 



Ethiopia is ruled by ( 'under the 
authority of r ) the emperor. 

'The book is under the table. f 



The postposition /sir/ 'under ! is used m combination with the prefix- 
preposition /k(9)-/, /M9)-/ or /1-/ interchangeably. Thus 'The book is under 
the table ' may be said /m9Sihafu koT9r9Pezaw sir new/, /meSihafu b9T9r9Pezaw 
sir n9w/ and /m9Sihafu iT9r9Pezaw sir n9w/.

Note £5.1 Postpositions. 

The structure sentences illustrate the use of the most common 
postpositions Used III combination With various prefix-prepositions. 

The majority of these postpositions were discussed m previous 

units. 

As can be seen from the above sentences most of the postpositions 
may be used m combination with two or three different prepositions inter- 
changeably. The use of this or that preposition is usually determined by 
local preference. Following is a summing up of the most used postpositions

Note £6.1 Noun* Irregular Plural 



woyzazirtinna mokwanmt C iff era 
mayQt yiw9ddallu. 

mokwamntinna mosafmt yommimsrT 
rasu now t 



'Ladies and gentlemen like to 
watch, popular dances', 

'Is he the one who appoints the 
notables and aristocrats? 



The 'irregular' plural m /-yan/ was discussed in Note 3.6. The above 
examples illustrate the use of three other irregular plurals, i.e. /makwamnt/ 
(singulars /mekonnin/) , /woyzazirt/ (singulars /w9yz9ro/) and /mssafint/ 
(singulars /m9sfm/)« Still other ^irregular' plural forms have occurred in 
this Course, e.g. /w9rat/ 'months' (singular /w9r/) , /K9sawist/ 'priests 1 
(singular /Kes/), /higgigat/ 'laws' (singular /higg/) , etc, All these plural 
forms have been borrowed from G-eez. 

Some nouns may have both regular /-oc(c)/ and Geez plural forms. In 
some cases these forms may be used interchangeably, m other cases they have 
different meanings. In colloquial Amharic the Geez plural forms are often 
combined with the regular suffix /-oc(c)/ thus forming a kind of 'double 1 
plural forms, e.g. /mokwanintoc/, /w9yzaz irtoc/, etc. 

Following is a list of nouns which may have either regular plural forms 
in /-oc(c)/, or Geez plural forms, or double plurals: 

Regular Plural Irregular Plural Double Plural 

weroc 'months f worat wsratoc 

gizewoc 'times' gizeyat gizeyatoc 

'days 1

Kesoc 



'priests '

yohannis 



What kind of courts are there 
m Ethiopia? 

The highest court [of the land] 
is called the Imperial Supreme 
Court of Ethiopia, 

[and] there are many High Courts 
under it. 

Next to these there are courts 
called 'Provincial Courts 1 . 



In every county, district and 
borough there are courts 
called county, district and 
borough courts. 

The High Court can reverse the 
decisions of the lower courts 
or declare them void. 



kobbede 



What is the basis of the 
Ethiopian laws? 



line 

The new Ethiopian code is based 
on the French and German legal 
system ( 'line ' ) .

a relative

Grammatical Notes 

Note 60.1 The affix /bU)-/ prefixed to the verb in the Perfective form and 
to the time counters: week, month, year. 

l9romanna Tirun9s b9t9CaCCu How many months after their 

b9smt9nflaw W9r t9gabbu. engagement did Lemma and 

Trunesh marry? 

bam9tacc9w t9gabbu. They married a year later. 



557 



UNIT 60 



AMHARIC 



When the affix /bU)-/ is prefixed both to the Perfective form of the 
verb and to the time counter (week, month, year), and a noun suffix pronoun 
is affixed to the counter, the resulting construction corresponds m meaning 
to English subordinate clauses of time m sentences such as: f so many weeks 
(months or years) after I (he, she, you, etc.) did so-and-so, I (he, she, you, 
etc.) did (ort will do) so-and-so. 

This construction is illustrated by the following example st 



lzzih bomoTTahu bewsre tamm9mhu. 



_I became ill a month after I 
came here. 



2. lzzih bsmoTTahu b9sost were 
tammemhu. 



_I became ill three months after 
I came here. 



3- lzzih bomoTTah bQweru tamm9mhu. 



I. became ill a month after you 
came here. 



l±. lzzih bemeTTah bssostonnaw 
war tammQmhu. 



1^ became ill three months after 
you came here. 



These examples show that when the actor of the mam and subordinate 
clauses is the same (sentences 1 and 2), the corresponding noun suffix 
pronoun is affuced to the time counter. When the subjects of the two 
clauses are different (sentences 3 and if) only the definite suffix is affixed 
to the time counter or its modifier (ordinal number or the question word 
/smtenrla/ ! how manieth'). It should be noted, however, that whenever the 
emphasis is on the time element, the definite suffix is affixed to the time 
counters, not the noun suffix pronoun. Thus the sentences 1 and 2 can be 
said: 

1. lzzih bomoTTahu b9W9ru tammemhu. A month after I came here I 

became ill. 

2. izzih borne TTahu besostsnnaw war Three months after I came here 

tammemhu. I became ill. 

Other time counters such as /seat/ T hour T , /Ken/ f day f , /magist/ 'next 
day f , etc. are used m these constructions, but only with the definite suffix, 
never with the noun suffix pronouns. 

Examples t 

bemoTTa bemagistu tammoms. 

bemoTTan behuletennaw seat 
misaccinm bellan. 

bem9TTa behul9t9nfiaw K9n ayyohut. 



'He became ill the next day after 
he had arrived ' . 

'We had our dinner two hours 
after we had arrived'. 

'I saw him the (next) day after 
he arrived. 1

Note 60.2 Verbi Classification. 



A complete classification of Amharic verbs is beyond the scope of this 
course. The following charts illustrate the types of verbs that have occurred 
in units 1-60. Two additional verb forms have been added: 

Frequentative Causative m /a-/ and Frequentative Causative in /as-/ 
e.g. /alobabbsse/ T to dress someone' and /aslQbabbes©/ r to cause to dress 1 . 

The verbs are given in their three basic forms only, i.e. Infinitive, 
Perfective and Imperf ective. 

Without being complete, however, these charts take care of the majority 
of the most frequently used types of Amharic verbs. 

The charts include the following types of verbs:

575 



UNIT 



AMHARIC 



Introduction to the Amharic Writing System 

Amharic is written with the Geez syllabary, augmented by a few symbols 
for syllables which occur m Amharic, but not m G-eez (characters for /s/, 
A/, /n/, /z/, /v/, etc.) 

Two of the characters represent vowel sounds; otherwise all characters 
represent a syllable--a consonant plus a specific vowel. 

The order is left to right. 

Each character has seven forms (traditionally called 'orders 1 ), to accord 
with, the number of vowels. The Amharic names of the seven orders arei 



I /gi'iz/ consonant + A/ 

II /ka'ib " + /u/ 

III /salis/ f 1 + /!/ 

IV /rabi/ » » + /a/ 

V /ham is/ ' 1 + /e/ 

VI /sadis/ ' ' + A/ 

VII /sabi/ » ' + /o/ 



In addition, there are a few characters which represent consonant plus 
/w/ plus vowel* /gwo/, /hwa/, /kwi/, etc., and a character for the consonant 
A/ plus vowel which may occur m some loanwords.

vo 



In a few instances, there are two or three characters for the same Amharic 
syllable. These characters contrasted with each other m Geez, but are more or 
less interchangeable m Amharic. 

To put it another way, there are alternative spellings for certain 
Amharic words. 

1) 1, 3 and 13, i.e. (J , ^f) and ^ are /ha/ 5 (J* , and 
are /hu/; ^/ , and ^ are /hi/, etc. 

2) 5 and 7, i.e. I/J and are /so/; l/J* and are /su/, etc. 

3) 16 and 20, i.e. h and ^ are /a/; ^ and are /u/, etc. 

i^.) 30 and 31, i.e. ft) and ^ are /Se/, /J. and ^ are /Su/, etc. 

Note that the vowel of /gi r iz/ and that of /rabi/ in ^/ > //) * ^ > 
h and ^ is the same, i.e. or Lj = /ha/ 

ff) or ft) = /ha/ 
^ or ^ - /ha/ 
h or h = /a/ 
0 or ^? » / a / 

Note also that m modern Amharic character 18 Yl is written m the first 
order /gi'iz/ only. Syllables /hu/, /hi/, /ha/, /he/, /hi/ and /ho/ are re- 
presented by 1, 3 or 13. 

All symbols listed under the sixth order (/sadis/) may represent either 
a consonant followed by the vowel /1/, or a consonant only, e.g. may be 
/hi/ or /h/; if) may be /bi/ or /b/; J may be /ni/ or /n/, etc.

The doubling of consonants is not indicated m the writing system mv * 

the word may be read /ale/ 'he said', or /alio/ 'there is'; foOfl 

/abay/ r a liar' or /abbay/ T Nile'; 7^ /gona/ 'yet 1 or /gonna/ 'Christmas', 

etc. The meaning is determined by the context. 

A closer examination of the Amharic Syllabary shows that the majority 
of characters are predictable modifications of their basic form, i.e. from 
the first order /gi'iz/. Thus the second order /ka f ib/ is characterized by 
a hooked dash /** / usually attached to the center of the right side of the 
character, e.g.
